(T-3)     WARNING:  Military CYPRES AADs require both a descent below 1,500 feet AGL and 115  feet  per  second  (6900  vvi)  descent  rate  threshold  before  it  will  deploy  the  reserve parachute.  Jumpmasters will program the CYPRES in flight if en route time exceeds one hour  to  the  drop  zone,  and  require  an  accurate  local  altimeter  setting  to  do  so.    A programmed  altimeter  setting  significantly  different  from  the  actual  local  altimeter  can result in unintended deployment of the reserve canopy or failure to deploy when needed, endangering MFF personnel.  Use of auto-pressurization can also cause activation of the CYPRES AAD just after take-off in certain situations. 17.19.5.5.  Safety Device.  Provide safety personnel with parachutes carried on board the aircraft.  (T-3)  They may use a restraint harness at their discretion, if available.  Provide a  restraint  harness  to  other  personnel  required  to  be  mobile  in  the  cargo  compartment.  (T-3) 17.19.5.6.  A  DD  Form  1748,  Joint  Airdrop  Inspection  Record,  will  be  accomplished prior to  all equipment  airdrops.  Completion, retention, and disposition of the form  will be  in  accordance  with  the  Air  Force  Joint  Instruction  (AFJI)  13-210,  Joint  Airdrop Inspection  Records,  Malfunction/Incident  Investigations,  and  Activity  Reporting,  as supplemented.    Exception:    A-7A  and  A-21  containers  rigged  for  door  bundles.    The aerial delivery unit supporting movement of equipment and supplies will ensure current publications are available to the loadmaster to ensure standardization of joint inspections.  (T-3)    Aerial  delivery  units  supporting  AFSOC  unilateral  airdrop  training  will  provide copies of required publications.  (T-3) 17.19.5.7.  In-Flight  Rigging  Procedures.    In-flight  rigging  is  a  safe  procedure  if accomplished  under  a  controlled  situation.    Accomplish  in-flight  rigging  only  when  the safety  of  the  personnel  required  to  be  mobile  in  the  cargo  compartment  is  not jeopardized.    (T-3)    The  aircraft  may  have  to  change  altitude.    Use  the  following procedures on airdrop missions that require in-flight rigging:  (T-3) AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015   211  17.19.5.7.1.  Two  loadmasters  are  required,  except  for  SATB.    At  least  one loadmaster will remain on interphone at all times.  (T-3) 17.19.5.7.2.  The use of the pendulum release system is optional for SATBs. 17.19.5.7.3.  Make  an  in-flight  check  of  the  rigged  items  using  the  DD  Form  1748, Joint Airdrop Inspection Record. 17.19.5.8.  Airspeed adjustments should be completed prior to slowdown.   WARNING:  For personnel airdrops, the pilot must be aware of paratroopers standing in the back and avoid drastic pitch, bank and power changes after the Six Minute Advisory.  (T-3)  Use of auto throttles may cause drastic speed changes resulting in an unstable platform. 17.19.5.9.  For paratroop door drops.  The PM opens the air deflector doors upon slowing below  150  knots  indicated  air  speed  (KIAS).    Once  air  deflector  doors  are  called  open, this automatically clears the LM to open the paratroop doors. 17.19.6.  IMC Drop. 17.19.6.1.  Drop Altitude.  Plan minimum IMC drop altitudes at MSA or as specified in AFI 11231, Computed Air Release Point Procedures, whichever is higher. 17.19.6.2.  As  defined  in  Federal  Aviation  Regulation  (FAR)  Exemption  4371L operations must be conducted at 500 feet above the highest obstacles within 3 nm of the course to be flown over mountainous or other areas.  (T-1)  Note:  Crews may segment drop corridor MSAs as mission requirements dictate. 17.19.6.2.1.  DZ Entry Point.  The point where an aircraft may safely begin  descent from IFR en route altitude or a segmented altitude to IMC drop altitude. 17.19.6.2.2.  DZ  Exit  Point.    A  fixed  point  on  the  DZ  escape  flight  path  centerline where  each  aircraft  will  be  at  minimum  IFR  en  route  altitude.    (T-3)    Normally, calculate the exit point based on a 1,000 feet per minute (FPM) climb at 140 KIAS.  (T-3)    Analyze  3-engine  climb  performance  and  ensure  obstacle  clearance  at  pre-airdrop  gross  weight.    (T-3)    If  unable  to  clear  all  obstacles  on  3engines,  adjust  the DZ  exit  point  based  on  3-engine  climb  capability.    (T-3)    This  point  will  be  a minimum  of  4  nm  track  distance  from  the  trailing  edge  of  the  DZ.    (T-3)    WARNING:    Analyze  pre-drop  gross  weight  to  determine  if  obstructions  can  be cleared on 3engines from DZ entry through DZ exit.  (T-3)  If obstructions cannot be cleared  reduce  aircraft  gross  weight,  revise  run-in  and/or  escape  course,  or  increase drop altitude. 17.19.7.  No-Drop Decisions. 17.19.7.1.  Prior  to  the  1  minute  warning  (2  min  for  high  altitude  combination  drops), notify the PIC when any condition exists that could jeopardize a safe drop.  (T-3) 17.19.7.2.  After the 1 minute warning (2 min for high altitude combination drops), any crew member observing a condition that would jeopardize a safe drop will transmit "No-drop" on the interphone.  (T-3) 17.19.7.3.  For  heavy  equipment  (HE)  airdrops  any  multifunction  control  display (MFCD)  ACAWS  occurring  after  the  1  minute  warning  time  advisory,  the  loadmaster will call a “Nodrop”.  (T-3)   212  AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015 17.19.7.4.  A “No-drop” will be called if all checklists through the 1 minute warning are not completed prior to the “Standby Drogue” advisory for HE towplate drops or by the “5 Second” call for all other drops.  (T-3) 17.19.7.4.1.  The  PM,  CSO,  and  LM  will  acknowledge  the  "No-drop"  call.    (T-3)  The  PM  will  place  the  Computer  Drop  switch  to  “Manual”  to  prevent  automatic release  of  the  load.    The  PF  will  immediately  instruct  the  crew  to  follow  the appropriate procedures.  (T-3) 17.19.7.4.2.  If a no-drop is called after the load restraint is removed and a racetrack is not planned, reapply the load restraint.  (T-3) 17.19.7.5.  Stopping  a  drop  in  progress.    Any  crew  member  recognizing  an  unsafe condition during an airdrop will state "red light".  (T-3)  The PM will turn on the red light to signal the airdrop termination.  (T-3) 17.19.7.6.  On personnel airdrops where surface winds are unknown, (such as unmanned DZs) advise the jumpmaster and Army airborne mission commander when drop altitude winds  exceed  30  knots  (KTS).    (T-3)    In  this  instance,  the  decision  to  drop  is  at  user's discretion. 17.19.8.  Departure from DZ (Escape).  The CSO will call “red light” at the expiration of the “green  light”  time.    (T-3)    When  the  LM  calls  “bags  in”,  immediately  begin  the  escape maneuver.    (T-3)    Do  not  exceed  140  KIAS  until  the  static  lines  are  retrieved  to  preclude entanglement.  (T-3)  During combat, static lines that cannot be retrieved will be cut so that doors can be closed.  (T-3)  Close all doors, raise flaps, and accelerate to en route airspeed.  (T3)    It  is  imperative  that  the  aircraft  be  configured  for  high  speed  evasive  maneuvers.  Therefore, after the static lines have been retrieved or cut, the doors and air deflectors closed, and  flaps  raised,  the  remainder  of  the  post-drop  checklist  may  be  deferred  to  a  more convenient time while returning to the recovery base.    WARNING:  Coordination between the  PF  and  Loadmaster  should  be  accomplished  prior  to  the  airdrop  to  prevent  injury  of aircrew in the back of the aircraft once the power levers are advanced to max power for the escape. 17.19.9.  Static Line Retrieval.  Loadmasters should allow a few moments for static lines to wrap together before retrieving static lines.  Designated aircrew members will retrieve static lines as soon as possible after parachutist or bundle exit is completed or exiting is suspended.  (T-3)  During combat, static lines that cannot be retrieved will be cut so doors can be closed.  (T-3)  If the static line retriever fails, static lines will be manually retrieved using a tie-down strap as follows.  (T-3)  Note:  A maximum of ten static lines may be retrieved manually per paratroop door.  (T-3)  A maximum of 20 static lines per cable may be retrieved with a static line retriever winch.  (T-3) 17.19.9.1.  Static Line Retriever alternate recovery procedures: 17.19.9.2.  The following procedures will be used when the Static Line Retrieval System is unavailable.  (T-3) 17.19.9.2.1.  Paratroop Door. 17.19.9.2.1.1.  Secure the hook end of a cargo strap to a point far enough forward in the cargo compartment to allow static lines to completely enter the aircraft.  (T-AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015   213  3) 17.19.9.2.1.2.  Insert the other end of the cargo strap from the bottom up, making a "U" around the static lines.  (T-3) 17.19.9.2.1.3.  Pull  the  strap  forward  to  retrieve  the  static  lines  into  the  aircraft.  (T-3)  Loadmasters may require assistance to pull the strap forward. 17.19.9.2.2.  Ramp and Door. 17.19.9.2.2.1.  If  the  static  line  retriever  fails  following  a  combination/tailgate drop, use the Prusik Knot as described below.  (T-3)  Refer to Figure 17.9, Prusik Knot. 17.19.9.2.2.2.  Take a 30 inch piece of 1/2-inch tubular nylon cord that is tied in a loop.  (T-3)  Loop the cord around the static line retriever cable as shown in the figure.  (T-3)  Use a minimum of three wraps around the retriever cable to ensure locking of the 1/2-inch tubular nylon cord.  (T-3) 17.19.9.2.2.3.  Pull to tighten the knot around the retriever winch cable to prevent slipping.  (T3)  Attach the hook end of a 5,000 pound strap into the loop's end and pull in the static lines.  (T-3)  The 1/2-inch tubular nylon cord will remain locked in place under tension.  (T-3) 17.19.9.2.2.4.  More than one 1/2 -inch tubular nylon cord may be attached to the winch  cable  using  the  Prusik  Knot  to  facilitate  static  line  retrieval.    The  Prusik Knot may be adjusted up or down the cable as required.  The knot will not slip as long  as  it  is  wrapped  tightly  with  a  minimum  of  three  turns  (more  wraps  equal more friction), and tension is applied. 17.19.9.2.2.5.  To  prevent  cutting  the  1/2-inch  tubular  nylon  cord  with  the  hook on  the  5,000  pound  strap,  a  carabineer  may  be  attached  to  the  1/2-inch  tubular nylon cord loop and the hook attached to carabineer.   214  AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015 Figure 17.9.  Prusik Knot.  17.19.10.  Airdrop  Malfunction  and  Emergencies.    Specific  emergency  procedures  for  each type  airdrop  are  located  in  the  TO  1C-130(E)J-1  checklists  or  the  appropriate  MAJCOM approved  guidance.    After  all  appropriate  emergency  actions  are  complete,  accomplish  the Completion  of  Drop  (COD)  checklist.    (T-3)    Note:    Prior  to  stations  time,  the  pilot  will ensure all crew members have reviewed the emergency procedures for the proposed airdrop.  (T-3)  The aircrew will conduct detailed emergency briefings.  (T-3)  Loadmasters will brief visual and verbal signals and establish coordinated tasks for their crew position prior to the first airdrop warning.  (T-3) 17.19.11.  Combination  Drops.    Combination  drops  are  when  parachutists  exit  after  the airdrop load.  The item requiring the highest altitude will determine the drop altitude.  (T-3) 17.19.11.1.  Restrictions.    A  maximum  of  20  personnel  static  lines may  be  deployed  on each pass and only one anchor cable will be used for these static lines.  (T-3)  Static lines AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015   215  for  equipment  will  be  attached  to  opposite  anchor  cables  and  must  be  rigged  for breakaway.  (T-3)  Exception:  When airdrop loads are rigged with pilot chutes packed in T-10 bags, they will be attached to the same anchor cable as personnel.  (T-3)  Maximum static lines per cable are 20.  (T-3)  Static lines will be retrieved after each pass. 17.19.11.2.  Procedures.    In  addition  to  the  equipment  CARP,  the  CSO  will  compute  a personnel  CARP  down  track  from  the  equipment  release  point  using  the  computed  exit time for the equipment drop as the interval between the equipment and personnel CARP.  (T-3)  Use the same airspeed and altitude as for the equipment for this computation.  (T-3)    If  the  computed  release  point  will  result  in  any  jumper  landing  within  150  yards  of any  boundary  of  the  DZ,  inform  the  jumpmaster.    (T-3)    Release  the  equipment  at  the equipment CARP, followed by the parachutists when the door is clear.  (T-3)  Exception:  For door and ramp bundles, use release points as specified in Paragraph 17.19.17.2.3. 17.19.12.  Low-Altitude Airdrops.  For detailed information, refer to AFTTP 3-3.E/MC-130J.  Refer to the expanded checklist for normal and emergency airdrop procedures. 17.19.13.  Personnel Drops. 17.19.13.1.  Aircraft Configuration.  The loadmaster will ensure the configuration of the anchor cables and seats are consistent with the number of personnel to be airdropped.  (T-3)  During aircraft preflight,  the loadmaster will ensure that all seats  have a serviceable retaining  strap  attached.    (T-3)    Fit  seats  without  a  serviceable  retaining  strap  with  a suitable length of type III nylon, premeasured for sufficient length to secure the seat in a raised position to the seat back support frame.  (T-3)  For single pass drops, ensure that parachutists have secured all seats (as required) and no part of the seat protrudes into the aisle.  (T-3)  On multiple passes, the number of parachutists to be dropped on that pass determines the number of seats raised.  (T-3)  Troop seats will be raised and secured, or lowered,  as  required,  by  personnel  under  the  supervision  and  instruction  of  the loadmaster.  (T-3)  Floor loading is authorized in accordance with Chapter 6. 17.19.13.2.  Aircraft Emergency During Personnel Airdrop.  When an aircraft emergency occurs  during  or  after  the  time  the  parachutists  stand  and  hookup,  the  following procedures will apply:  (T-3) 17.19.13.2.1.  Under  acceptable  conditions  when  parachutists’  bailout  is  required, maintain  an  acceptable  altitude  and  attitude  for  the  parachutists  to  evacuate  the aircraft.    (T-3)    The  minimum  acceptable  altitude  is  as  briefed.    (T-3)    If  the  jump airspeed will be in excess of 150 KIAS/Knots Calibrated Air Speed (KCAS), advise the parachutists of the airspeed and altitude.  (T-3)  Order evacuation of the aircraft by giving the pre-briefed bailout emergency signals.  (T-3) 17.19.13.2.1.1.  Minimum emergency bail-out altitude for free-fall parachutists is 2,000 feet AGL.  (T-3) 17.19.13.2.2.  When conditions are not acceptable for parachutists’ bailout or a drop is aborted for other reasons, the following procedures apply:  (T-3) 17.19.13.2.2.1.  The  red  light  will  be  turned  "ON"  and  will  remain  on  until  exit doors are closed.  (T-3) 17.19.13.2.2.2.  The  PF  will  advise  the  loadmaster,  who  in  turn  will  advise  the   216  AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015 jumpmaster  to  have  the  parachutists  unhook,  take  their  seats,  and  fasten  safety belts.  (T-3) 17.19.14.  Container Delivery System.  Container delivery system is designed to airdrop A-22/A-23,  A-7,  A-21,  double  A-22/A-23  containers  and  loads  rigged  on  combat  expendable platforms.  The weight of each container and type parachute used should be IAW  TO 13C7-series/Field Manual 10-501 and USSOCOM 350-series rigging manuals. 17.19.14.1.  CDS  rigging  procedures.    Utilize  rigging  procedures  outlined  in    TO 1C130J9.    The  following  describes  the  types  of  CDS  releases  that  may  be  used  on AFSOC EC130J aircraft. 17.19.14.2.  Normal  System  Release.    A  normal  system  release  utilizes  the  static  line retriever and guillotine knife to cut/release the CDS gate.  When using this method, the loadmaster  may  assist  the  release  by  pulling  down  sharply  on  the  static  line  retriever winch cable. 17.19.14.3.  Manual Gate Cut.  A manual gate cut is defined as using a knife or V blade type knife to cut/release the CDS gate for all single stick container drops.  Loads rigged with  type  XXVI/VIII  nylon  not  exceeding  5,000  may  be  manually  cut  using  these procedures. 17.19.14.4.  Combat Cut.  A combat cut is defined as using a knife or V blade type knife to cut the CDS gate regardless of amount, weight, and configuration of CDS on board.  It is  used  only  when  authorized  as  part  of  specific  exercise  operations  order  or  plan, contingencies, or by COMAFSOF.  (T-3) 17.19.15.  CDS/CRRC/RAMZ  Specific  Procedures:          WARNING:    The  aircraft  tends  to pitch up as the load exits the aircraft.  This pitch must be anticipated and controlled to allow no more than 2 or 3°additional pitch.  (T-3)  Do not  over control to  the point that negative gravity G forces are encountered.  (T-3) 17.19.15.1.  Escape.  Departing the DZ, set flaps to 50%, accelerate to en route airspeed, climb or descend as briefed and perform the completion of drop checklist.  (T-3)  Flaps may be called up at the discretion of the PF in lieu of going to 50%. 17.19.15.2.  Combat  Rubber  Raiding  Craft  Drop.    CRRCs  may  be  dropped  in  a  stacked configuration  allowing  up  to  four  CRRCs  to  be  dropped  on  a  single  pass.    One  CRRC platform  can  be  dropped  using  the  standard  CDS  or  container  recovery  system  (CRS) checklist.    When  two  CRRC  platforms  are  dropped  on  a  single  pass,  use  the  CDS checklist.  (T-3)  Two CRRC platforms with up to 18 personnel or one CRRC platform with  up to  19 personnel  can be airdropped on one pass.   Rigged  IAW TO 13C7-51.   If aircraft  gross  weight  exceeds  120,000  pounds,  use  140  KIAS/KCAS  CDS/CRS  flap setting chart.  (T-3)  Exception:  For combination drops above 120,000 pounds use 130 KIAS/KCAS and 130 KCAS flap setting chart.  (T-3) 17.19.15.2.1.  Emergency Procedures.  Use CDS airdrop emergency procedures. 17.19.15.2.2.  Training and Exercises.  One or more recovery boats will be in position to recover platforms, parachutes, and personnel.  (T-3)  The boat should be displaced 400 yards or more from the DZ axis.  The CSO or PM can confirm the release point and offset distance visually or by using radar returns. AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015   217  17.19.15.2.3.  DZ Axis.  If possible, the DZ axis will be into the drop altitude wind (± 30°)  when  the  wind  is  5  knots  or  greater.    The  jumpmaster  will  be  advised  if  this cannot be done. 17.19.15.2.4.  Backup  Drop  Platform.    The  raiding  party  exits  when  the  pilot  chute deploys.  In operational employments, a second platform may be carried as a backup in  case  the  main  chute  fails  following  pilot  chute  deployment.    Backup  platform release  point  and  aircrew  or  raiding  party  communication  and  procedures  will  be coordinated prior to the mission.  Procedures and tactics will also be coordinated for airdrop of a second platform and party, if applicable. 17.19.15.2.5.  The following will apply when dropping two CRRC platforms on one pass:  (T-3) 17.19.15.2.5.1.  The forward release gate must be manually cut by the loadmaster.  (T-3) 17.19.15.2.5.2.  The second loadmaster must be positioned in  the paratroop door prior to completion of the ten-minute warning.  (T-3) 17.19.15.2.5.3.  NVG are required for the airdrop of two CRRC platforms on one pass at night.  (T-3) 17.19.15.3.  Rigging Alternate Method Zodiac.  The RAMZ consists of a deflated Zodiac F470  combat  rubber  raiding  craft.    The  RAMZ  engine  is  configured  in  a  plywood  box and secured in a standard A-22 container using two T-10 parachutes.  When rigged, the RAMZ package weighs between 600 and 1,000 pounds. 17.19.15.3.1.  Emergency Procedures.  Follow either the CDS emergency procedures or fouled parachutist procedures. 17.19.15.3.2.  Aircraft  Preparation.    Aircraft  preparation  will  be  in  accordance  with 1C-130J-9.    The  RAMZ  package  will  be  delivered  rigged  and  prepared  for  loading.  The  loadmaster  is  responsible  for  aircraft  rigging,  attaching  the  static  line,  and securing  the  package.    (T-3)    When  the  bundle  is  used  in  conjunction  with  SAR operations, it is not uncommon for the Special Tactics Squadron (STS) or Pararescue Jumper (PJ) team to require the flexibility to determine method of deployment (static line  or  MFF)  after  they  determine  weather  conditions  at  the  recovery  site.    In  this case,  the  loadmaster  should  prepare  the  aircraft  to  allow  for  either  option  and complete  all  bundle  rigging  with  the  exception  of  static  line  attachment.    In-flight prior  to  the  20  minute  warning,  the  team  along  with  the  crew  will  determine  the method of deployment.  (T3)  At this point, the loadmaster will attach the static line to the  appropriate  anchor  cable  and  comply  with  In-Flight  Rigging  Procedures.    (T-3)  The  package  is  rigged  and  dropped  using  normal  CDS  combination  procedures,  or rigged on the ramp using ramp bundle procedures. 17.19.15.3.3.  Deployment.    Checklist  items  up  to  and  including  the  six-minute advisory  may  be  accomplished  prior  to  identifying  the  target.    Accomplish  the slowdown using normal slowdown procedures.  (T-3) 17.19.15.3.4.  DZ  Axis.    If  possible,  the  DZ  axis  will  be  into  the  wind  (±  30°)  for MFF  drops  if  drop  altitude  wind  is  5  knots  or  greater.    The  jumpmaster  will  be   218  AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015 advised when this cannot be complied with.  Release for static line deployments can be either into the wind or downwind. 17.19.15.3.5.  Release  Point.    The  CSO  determines  the  release  point.    (T-3)    If  the bundle is rigged using ramp bundle procedures the loadmaster will manually cut the RAMZ  bundle  loose  which  will  signal  the  jumpers  they  are  clear  to  jump.    (T-3)  MFF  jumpers  will  exit  approximately  6  seconds  after  load  release.    Static  line jumpers  will  exit  immediately  after  the  load  release.    (T-3)        WARNING:    MFF jumpers will not exit until RAMZ static lines have been retrieved.  (T-3) 17.19.16.  Heavy  Equipment  Drops  (HE).    Only  equipment  rigged  in  accordance  with  TO 13C7 series and Field Manual 10-500 series or USSOCOM 350-series rigging manuals will be  airdropped.    Nonstandard  equipment  and  loads  require  specific  instructions  and  waivers from HQ AFSOC/A3V prior to airdrop.  (T-2) 17.19.17.  Door Bundle.  A7A or A-21 containers weighing up to 500 pounds are referred to as door bundles and may be dropped through the paratroop door or ramp and door using the personnel airdrop checklist.  Door bundles may be dropped independently or in conjunction with  personnel.    Bundle  airdrops  are  limited  to  one  bundle  per  paratroop  door.    When dropped  with  personnel,  the  bundle  will  be  the  first  object  to  exit  the  aircraft.    (T-3)    The maximum bundle size in inches is 66 (height) x 48 (width) x 30 (length) for paratroop door loads.  (T-3)  Joint airdrop inspection (JAI) is not required for door bundles. 17.19.17.1.  Paratroop  Doors  (Personnel  Checklist).    Remove  restraint  and  position  the bundle in the paratroop doors prior to completion of the Run-In checklist.  (T-3)  Ensure door bundles are not equipped with breakaway static lines.  (T-3) 17.19.17.2.  Ramp and Door: 17.19.17.2.1.  Door  Bundle  (Personnel  Checklist).    Door  bundles  dropped  off  the ramp  and  door  will  be  equipped  with  breakaway  static  lines  in  accordance  with  TO 13C7-1-11, or with parachutes packed in T-10 bags.  Door bundles dropped from the ramp and door that exceed the weight or dimensions specified in Paragraph 17.19.17 will be equipped with a skid board compatible with the intermediate roller conveyors. 17.19.17.2.2.  Ramp Bundle (Personnel or CDS Checklist).  Load dimensions will be in accordance with above referenced directives.  The total rigged weight  of the load will not exceed 2,335 lbs.  Ramp bundles will be rigged in accordance with TO 13C7-1-11, Field Manual 10-500, and USSOCOM 350-series directives and dropped as on-the-ramp  loads.    Remove  vertical  and  aft  restraint  at  the  10-minute  warning.    (T-3)  Remove forward restraint prior to the completion of the Run-In checklist.  (T-3)  The loads will be released by the loadmaster and may be followed by parachutists.  (T-3)  These  procedures  can  be  accomplished  from  either  side  of  the  cargo  ramp  or  from centerline.  Specific aircraft preparations and rigging procedures are contained in TO 1C-130J-9. 17.19.17.2.3.  Release  Point.    When  door/ramp  bundles  are  dropped  with  personnel, compute a personnel CARP.  (T-3)  Compute an additional CARP for the door bundle and plot the vectors from  the personnel  release  point to  ensure it will impact  within the  DZ  boundaries.    (T-3)    Release  the  bundle  at  the  personnel  CARP,  followed  by the  parachutists  when  the  door  is  clear.    (T-3)    An  alternate  procedure  is  to  add  the AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015   219  exit time of the bundle to the personnel  CARP, and release the bundle at  this point, followed by the jumpers.   If this method is  used, the crew must coordinate with  the user to ensure the load lands at the desired location.  When a door bundle is the only object being dropped, base the release on the CARP for the bundle.  (T-3) 17.19.17.3.  Only equipment rigged in accordance with TO 13C7-series and Field Manual 10500  series  or  USSOCOM  350-series  rigging  manuals,  properly  marked  with  accurate weights and shipper’s declarations will be airdropped.  Nonstandard equipment and loads require specific instructions and waivers from HQ AFSOC/A3V.  (T-2) 17.19.18.  Joint Precision Airdrop System Procedures. 17.19.18.1.  The  term  JPADS  refers  to  GPS-guided  equipment,  Improved  Container Delivery  System  (I-CDS)  and  MFF  airdrops  that  utilize  the  JPADS  Mission  Planner (JPADS-MP) system. 17.19.18.2.  JPADS  Restrictions.    Wind  data  derived  from  the  JPADS  four  dimensional wind  model  is  suitable  for  mission  use,  but  the  JPADS-MP  does  not  calculate  MFF HARP correctly.  AFSOC crews will not use JPADS-MP as the sole source for personnel MFF HARP calculations until authorized.  (T-3)  Aircrews will use sound judgment and evaluate  the  dropsonde  derived  winds,  forecasted  winds,  and  preflight  HARP  location before conducting MFF operations.  (T-3) 17.19.18.3.  JPADS  certified  aircrews  are  authorized  to  use  the  JPADS-MP  laptop  and software (a stand-alone system) to calculate release points for JPADS operations. 17.19.18.3.1.  MFF airdrops only, aircrews can input dropsonde collected winds into onboard airdrop systems, utilize the Air Force Weather Agency forecast/model during mission planning, and incorporate dropsonde collected winds when calculating HARP in AFSOC approved airdrop planning software for personnel. 17.19.18.3.2.  The  collected  winds  may  be  entered  into  the  wind/temperature summary  layer  for  use  in  HARP  calculation.    Note:    JPADS  winds  are  collected  in magnetic  or  true  so  winds  may  require  conversion  to  true  before  entry  into  mission computers. 17.19.18.4.  JPADS  certified  crews  are  authorized  JPADS  airdrop  operations  in  VMC and  IMC  above  3,000  feet  AGL  without  the  use  of  a  Radar  Beacon  or  Ground  Radar Aerial Delivery System (GRADS). 17.19.18.5.  Drop zone size criteria during contingency operations are at the discretion of the user.  Ultimately, the user accepts responsibility for the accuracy and the condition of these airdrop loads. 17.19.18.6.  The  planned  release  point  for  actual  JPADS  training  airdrops  must  be executed within the lateral and vertical limits of a restricted area.  (T-3) 17.19.18.7.  During the dropsonde release, use of  zero flaps at  speeds between 170–180 KIAS  is  required to  preclude dropsonde tail  strikes.   (T-3)   Loadmasters  should release the dropsondes from the corners of the cargo ramp.   220  AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015 17.19.18.8.  Surface  Wind  Limits.    Surface  wind  limitations  are  unrestricted  for dropsonde operations, 18 knots for Firefly, Screamer, and Dragonfly and as published for all other parachutes. 17.19.18.9.  Altitude Restrictions.  (T-3) 17.19.18.9.1.  I-CDS/JPADS:  Maximum altitude 25,000 ft MSL.  (T-3) 17.19.18.9.2.  SCREAMER minimum drop altitude is 8,000 ft AGL.  (T-3) 17.19.18.9.3.  FIREFLY minimum drop altitude is 5,000 ft AGL.  (T-3) 17.19.18.9.4.  Dropsonde minimum drop altitude is 3,000 ft AGL.  (T-3) 17.19.18.10.  For all training drops the dropsonde will be planned to land in the intended impact/drop  area  using  the  release  point  determined  by  the  JPADS-MP.    (T-3)    The planned release point will be programmed in the CNI-MU into the mission computer as a normal (non-drop) leg.  (T-3)  Aircraft position will be confirmed either by GPS with a Figure(s) of Merit (FOM) of 3 or better or via aircraft sensor updates.  (T-3) 17.19.18.11.  Equipment  Drops.    CSO  will  program  the  planned  release  point  into  the mission computer as a normal (non-drop) leg.  (T-3)  Aircraft position will be confirmed either by GPS with a FOM of 3 or better or via aircraft sensor updates.  (T-3)  For non-GPS  guided  drops  CSOs  will  validate  the  JPADS-MP  release  point  via  normal  CARP procedures.  (T-3) 17.19.18.11.1.  A  drop  leg  may  be  used  with  SONDE  derived  winds  for  unguided JPADS  equipment  drops  where  parachute  ballistic  data  is  listed  in  accordance  with AFI 11-231, Computed  Air Release Point Procedures.  Aircrew will ensure that the aircraft  calculated  release  point  falls  within  the  JPADS  mission  planner  calculated LAR.  (T-3) 17.19.19.  Low  Cost  Aerial  Delivery  System  (LCADS):    The  Low  Cost  Aerial  Delivery System  is  a  one-time  use,  stand-alone  airdrop  system  consisting  of  a  modular  suite  of  low cost airdrop items, and be comprised of parachutes, containers, platforms, and other air items configured for low-velocity, high-velocity, and free fall-drop aerial delivery of loads. 17.19.20.  Low Cost Aerial Delivery System-Low Velocity (LCADS-LV).  Use CDS airdrop checklists and flap settings.  (T-3) 17.19.20.1.  LCADS-LV  Ballistics.    LCADS-LV  will  be  dropped  using  the  ballistics IAW  AFI  11-231  or  published  Air  Transportability  Test  Loading  Agency  (ATTLA) memorandum.    Exit  times  will  be  IAW  AFI  11-231,  Computed  Air  Release  Point Procedures. 17.19.20.1.1.  Drop  Zone  Size.    The  drop  zone  size  will  be  IAW  AFI  13-217,  Drop Zone  and  Landing  Zone  Operations.    The  minimum  reliable  drop  altitude  is  1,000 feet  AGL  (drops  below  800  feet  AGL  pose  a  significant  risk  of  failure  or  damage).  The  maximum  drop  altitude  is  13,000  feet  MSL  (drops  above  10,000  feet  MSL  are restricted to a maximum of 1,700 lbs suspended weight).  (T-3) 17.19.20.2.  Low  Cost  Aerial  Delivery  System-High  Velocity  (LCADS-HV).    Use  CDS airdrop checklists and flap settings.  (T-3) AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015   221  17.19.20.2.1.  LCADS-HV  is  not  authorized  for  training  use.    LCADS-HV  is authorized  for  contingency  missions  and  developmental/Developmental  Test  and Evaluation/(OT&E) missions. 17.19.20.2.2.  For  JPADS  qualified  crews,  LCADS-HV  is  currently  included  in  the JPADS  mission  planner.    When  using  the  JPADS  mission  planner  to  compute  an  I-CDS  release  point  and  utilizing  LCADS-HV  parachutes,  ensure  the  chute  type selected is “LCADSHV”.  (T-3) 17.19.20.2.3.  Minimum reliable drop altitude is 3,000 feet AGL.  IAW AFI 13-217, Paragraph  2.3,  drop  altitudes  below  3,000  AGL  may  be  required  due  to contingency/wartime  operations.    If  supported  and  supporting  forces  commanders require a drop altitude lower than 3,000 feet AGL, the drop altitude will be no lower than  1,800  feet  AGL.    (T-3)    This  absolute  minimum  drop  altitude  is  based  on historical success rates and recommendations by ballistics experts at NATICK. 17.19.20.2.4.  Maximum drop altitude is 25,000 feet MSL.  (T-3) 17.19.20.3.  Low Cost Aerial Delivery System-Low-Altitude (LCLA).  Use CDS airdrop checklists and 50% flap setting.  (T-3) 17.19.20.3.1.  LCLA  is  one  of  the  Army's  low  cost  airdrop  systems.    LCLA  was designed  to  provide  very  low-altitude  aerial  resupply  using  small  fixed  wing  and rotary wing aircraft.  LCLA is similar to the low cost airdrop system in that it is new low  cost  equipment,  but  LCLA  does  not  have  the  same  airdrop  parameters  as  low velocity  (LCADS-LV)  or  high  velocity  (LCADS-HV).    LCLA  was  designed  to provide  very  low-altitude  (150  -  300  feet  AGL)  aerial  resupply  using  small  fixed-wing and rotary-wing aircraft.  LCLA parachutes are compatible with A-7A/Low cost polypropylene straps, and A-21  containers.   Containers may  be  rigged with  a single parachute or with clusters of up to three parachutes. 17.19.20.3.2.  Aircrews  are  authorized  to  conduct  LCLA  airdrops  during  exercises, unilateral training, and contingency operations. 17.19.20.3.3.  All AFSOC C-130J variants will conduct LCLA airdrops at 130 KIAS using  personnel/container  ramp  load  checklist  procedures.    (T-3)    (Exceptions:    1.  Use 50% flaps for all LCLA drops.  (T-2)  Position anchor cable stops at FS 803.  (T-3) 17.19.20.3.4.  Surface  wind  limits  for  LCLA  drops  is  18  KTS.   Surface  wind  above this limit is at the discretion of supported unit commander. 17.19.20.3.5.  LCLA  drop  altitude  will  be  no  lower  than  150  feet  AGL  during  the day.    (T-3)    The  minimum  night  drop  altitude  is  300  feet.    (T-3)    Descent  to  drop altitude  will  begin  no  earlier  than  the  IP.    Situation  permitting,  notify  the  user  if airdropping above the expected LCLA altitude range (150-300 feet AGL). 17.19.20.3.6.  If the ramp cannot accommodate the number or weight of bundles to be dropped in one pass, rig and drop from the cargo floor using standard CDS checklists and rigging procedures.  (T-3)  Use the LCLA ballistic data from section 4A with the CDS exit times listed in AFI 11231.  Manual gate release is authorized. 17.20.  Receiver Air-to-Air Refueling.  Conduct IAW TO 1C-130(E)J-1 and ATP-56(B).   222  AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015 17.20.1.  Communication.    Unless  directed  otherwise  by  a  specific  exercise  operations order/plan, or during contingencies, communication capability between tankers and receivers will be maintained during all normal rendezvous and air refueling operations.  (T-3)  Voice transmissions  will  be  IAW  the  emission  control option  used.        CAUTION:    Air  refueling operations will not be conducted when radio communications capability between the tanker and receiver is lost, except during emergency fuel situation.  (T-3)  If radio communications are  lost,  or  unreadable  between  the  boom  operator  and  receiver  pilot,  contacts  will  not  be attempted.  (T-3) 17.20.2.  Use  manual  boom  latching  procedures  only  during  fuel  emergencies  and contingency operations.  (T-3)  Exception:  Manual boom latching procedures are authorized for all refueling operations with the KC-10 if the tanker's independent disconnect system is operational. 17.20.3.  Altitude Reservations (ALTRV).  Whenever practical, refueling operations are done on  tracks  or  anchor  areas  published  in  the  DoD  FLIP.    ALTRVs  are  used  when  certain missions  or  operational  considerations  may  require  air  refueling  operations  in  areas  not published in FLIP.  For specific ALTRV procedures, refer to FLIP and FAA Special Military Operations 7610.4H, 3. 17.20.4.  Special  Operations  Air  Refueling  (SOAR)  Procedures. 33.E/MC130J for specific procedures.   Refer  to  AFTTP AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015   223  Chapter 18 LEAFLET AIRDROP 18.1.  General.  Leaflet airdrops are accomplished in both hostile and permissive environments. 18.2.  Mission  Description.  Mission  requirements  vary  widely  depending  on  the  volume  of leaflets  airdropped,  leaflet  packaging,  and  the  altitude  of  the  airdrop.    Loads  will  normally  not exceed 25,000 pounds.  Drop altitudes may vary from 500 ft AGL to the aircraft service ceiling.  Leaflet  drops  require  the  movement  of  boxes  of  various  weights  from  throughout  the  cargo compartment via intermediate rollers (if required) to the ramp area for dispersal off the ramp.  If specific  mission  requirements  dictate  deviation  from  information  contained  in  this  publication, modify procedures to  meet  specific requirements.   Note:  All  airdrops conducted above FL250 require a waiver to AFI 11-202, Vol 3.  Long duration high altitude drops will require the aircraft remain open.  Consider outside air temperature and clothing requirements prior to step.  (T-2) 18.3.  Equipment.  In  addition  to  normal  aircraft  equipment,  the  following  items  may  be required to perform leaflet drops: 18.3.1.  Parachutes  or  restraining  harnesses  must  be  available  for  use  by  all  personnel working in the cargo compartment.  (T-3)  Normally six are required. 18.3.2.  A portable oxygen console with a minimum of four regulators and output hoses with sufficient length to reach throughout the entire cargo compartment must be available for high altitude airdrops (above 10,000 feet). 18.3.3.  An  interval  timer  is  desirable  (alternating  red  and  green  jump  lights  may  serve  for sequencing the drops). 18.4.  Aircraft  Configuration.  Determine  if  leaflets  are  floor  loaded  (on  aircraft  dual  rail intermediate conveyors) or are configured on pallets.  Based on how the leaflets are prepared and the volume of leaflets to airdrop, there are several possible configurations. 18.4.1.  Palletized Loads.  Preload large volumes of leaflets on pallets and load as palletized cargo.    Resources  permitting,  this  is  the  recommended  procedure  for  large  volumes  of leaflets. 18.4.1.1.  Use modified pallets with warehouse rollers attached to the top of the pallets in two sticks. 18.4.1.2.  Stack  the  leaflet  boxes  on  the  pallets  in  two  sticks  and  secure  them  to  the pallets before loading aircraft. 18.4.1.3.  In addition to the modified pallets, provide a bridge to span the gap between the last floor pallet and the ramp pallet. 18.4.1.3.1.  Construct the bridge so the rollers on it are the same height as the rollers on top of the pallets when loaded on the aircraft dual rails (EC-130J). 18.4.1.3.2.  Install  the  bridge  after  the  ramp  is  lowered  to  the  airdrop  position  and before completion of the slowdown or 3-minute checklist.   224  AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015 18.4.2.  Floor  Loads.    Leaflets  may  also  be  loaded  directly  on  the  aircraft  intermediate conveyors,  if  available.    Employ  this  procedure  for  smaller  loads  of  leaflets  or  when  the equipment mentioned above is not available. 18.4.2.1.  Individual boxes have to be hand loaded and each group of boxes individually secured using aircraft tie-down equipment. 18.4.2.2.  Configure the aircraft IAW the applicable volume and addenda. 18.4.2.2.1.  Recommend  the  intermediate  conveyors  be  moved  inboard  to  the  high speed configuration if size of the load permits. 18.4.2.2.2.  If the intermediate conveyors must remain in the outboard configuration, exercise care during loading to ensure the individual boxes contact two intermediate rollers.    The  boxes  may  not  roll  or  may  be  damaged  if  loaded  on  only  one  roller conveyor. 18.4.2.2.2.1.  If  the  intermediate  conveyors  must  remain  in  the  outboard configuration,  consider  using  CDS  skid  boards  or  sheets  of  plywood  as  skids under the leaflet boxes. 18.4.2.2.2.2.  If  using  this  option,  secure  each  skid  of  leaflets  individually  using aircraft tie-down equipment. 18.4.2.2.2.3.  Drift each skid back to the ramp hinge during the airdrop, exercising caution not to let the skid board exit the aircraft. 18.4.2.2.2.4.  Secure  the  empty  skids  against  the  side  wall  until  the  airdrop  is completed. 18.5.  Preparation for Loading.  Complete applicable portions of the appropriate TO 1C-130J-9 checklists as follows: 18.5.1.  Cargo Loading Preparation. 18.5.2.  Cargo Loading (Palletized/Floor Load). 18.5.3.  General Airplane Preparation for Ramp and Door Airdrop/Extraction. 18.5.4.  Anchor Cables.  Rig one anchor cable on each side of the aircraft. 18.5.4.1.  Attach the aft latch of each anchor cable to the inboard U-bolt on the aft anchor cable support on each side of the airplane. 18.5.4.2.  Position  the anchor cable stops to  the very forward or  aft end of the cable, or remove and stow the stops, unless they are used as stops for the restraint harness restraint strap. 18.5.4.3.  Attach  the  forward  latch  to  the  center  U-bolts  on  the  forward  anchor  cable support. 18.5.4.3.1.  If the center U-bolts are not  accessible because of aircraft configuration, attach the forward latch to the outboard U-bolts. 18.5.4.3.2.  If  connecting  the  forward  latch  to  the  outboard  U-bolts,  leave  additional slack in the cable to allow anchor cable installation in the outboard location on the A-frame during the airdrop. AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015   225  18.5.4.3.3.  Lower  the  center  anchor  cable  supports  and  lock  them  in  the  vertical position with  the tubular supports.  Do not  connect  the anchor  cable to  the A-frame during  rigging.    The  cable  may  block  the  cargo  door  from  properly  opening  and closing. 18.5.4.3.4.  The  aft  anchor  cable  support  arm  micro  switches  should  be  isolated  so that the cargo ramp will operate with the anchor cables lowered. 18.5.4.3.4.1.  Isolate  the  micro  switches  by  securing  them  back  with  1/4-inch cotton webbing or gutted Type III nylon. 18.5.4.3.4.2.  Test the ramp for operation with the micro switches secured and the anchor cables lowered to ensure the ties were properly installed. 18.5.5.  Static  Line  Retrievers.    Suspend  oxygen  hoses  from  static  line  retriever  cables  for high altitude missions (not required if airdropping below 10,000 feet MSL). 18.5.5.1.  Unwind static line retriever cable(s). 18.5.5.2.  Install quick disconnects on the aft end of cable(s). 18.5.5.3.  Secure the quick disconnects to the inboard side of the A-frame with two turns of  1/4inch  cotton  webbing.    Make  this  tie  approximately  18  inches  above  the  anchor cable  attaching  point.    If  the  static  line  retriever  is  inadvertently  rewound,  the  tie  will break and will prevent damage to the A-frame or static line retriever. 18.5.5.4.  Remove all slack from static line retriever cables. 18.5.6.  Oxygen  Hoses  and  Interphone  Cord.    Tape  or  tie  extended  oxygen  hoses  and interphone  cords  together  and  suspend  from  static  line  retriever  cables  with  G-13  clevis  or suitable substitute. 18.5.6.1.  Ensure that approximately 12 G-13 clevises or suitable substitutes are available to tie oxygen hoses to the static line retriever cable(s). 18.5.6.2.  Recommend the kicker use the oxygen regulator and interphone cord aft of the left paratroop door.  Route the cord and hose up and over the overhead structural beams, and  back  down  next  to  the  overhead  ADS  release.    This  technique  keeps  the  hose  and cord off the floor preventing them from  becoming tangled or damaged.   It also  keeps it out of the kicker's way while kicking boxes and retrieving static lines. 18.6.  Securing Boxes.  Secure five boxes on the ramp for airdrop.  If loading preloaded pallets, two sticks of five boxes each, are secured on the ramp pallet. 18.7.  Connecting Static Lines.  Connect static lines to the anchor cable aft of the A-frame. 18.8.  Restraint  Strapping.  Place a single length of Type III nylon, as final restraint, over the stick of boxes.  The cargo restraint strap over the boxes on the ramp is removed at the 1-minute warning.  The Type III nylon final restraint is cut at the release point.   226  AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015 Figure 18.1.  Leaflet Box Rigging Procedures. 18.9.  Leaflet  Box  Rigging  Procedures.  Rigging  boxes dissemination. for  high-altitude  static line  18.9.1.  Using a punch, cut four holes approximately 2 1/2 inches by 3/4 inches as indicated in Figure  21.1.   If a punch is not available, a knife can be used.  When using a punch, the box  must  first  be  placed  upon  an  object  slightly  deeper  and  smaller  than  the  box  so  that  a hard surface is provided when striking the punch. 18.9.2.  Cut through the box from one hole to another as illustrated in Figure 21.1. 18.9.3.  Place  a  2-inch  wide  length  of  webbing  through  the  holes.    The  length  of  webbing depends upon the size of the box, but must be long enough to tie after the box has been filled with leaflets. 18.9.4.  Place a 3 or 4-inch strip of masking tape over the 2-inch wide webbing. 18.9.5.  Cut all four corners of the box from the top to about 3/4  of the way down (or less). 18.9.6.  Again  using  masking  tape,  tape  one  strip  up  the  cut  portion  and  two  across,  as illustrated. 18.9.7.  After filling the box with leaflets, tie the two strips of webbing. 18.9.8.  Attach  one  end  of  the  static  line  to  the  webbing  ties.    The  length  of  the  static  line depends upon the type of aircraft, but it is normally about 15 feet long.  The box is rolled out AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015   227  of  the  aircraft  and  as  the  container  comes  to  the  end  of  the  static  line,  the  sides  of  the  box split.  In effect, it is turned inside out.  The leaflets fall away followed by the empty box. 18.9.9.  This  is  one  method,  and  may  not  be  the  method  suited  for  your  mission.    Other variations do exist; however, this is the most universal and least complex. 18.10.  Preparation  for  Airdrop.  After  takeoff,  take  the  following  actions  before  starting  the leaflet airdrop checklist. 18.10.1.  Tape all ramp control panel switches, except the auxiliary pump and ramp switch. 18.10.2.  Lower  anchor  cables  and  connect  them  to  the  outboard  side  of  the  anchor  cable supports. 18.10.3.  Safety tie the aft anchor cable support micro switches. 18.10.4.  Position/don emergency equipment and survival gear. 18.10.5.  Begin Leaflet Airdrop checklist. 18.11.  Aircrew  Procedures.  Under  normal  circumstances  the  crew  required  for  leaflet operations consists of a tactical crew plus two Physiological Technician(s) (PT) (for high altitude airdrops) and three additional crew members to act as feeders.  The senior loadmaster will assign specific positions for activity in the cargo compartment.  (T-3) 18.11.1.  Drop  Configuration/Maneuvering.    Base  configuration  on  desired  coverage  and airframe  airspeed  limitations.    (T-3)    All  personnel  should  be  constantly  aware  of  the possibility of an emergency break upon encountering a threat. 18.11.1.1.  Flaps.  Flaps are normally set at 50% and flap settings should not be changed during  the  drop  unless  the  loadmaster  is  notified.    Note:    At  very  high  altitudes,  the aircraft  may  not  be  able  to  maintain  drop  airspeed  with  50%  flaps.    Under  these conditions, use an intermediate flap setting that allows for aircraft performance and also produces an acceptable deck angle for the drop.  A flap setting near 20% gives the best aircraft wing performance while providing high rudder boost for controllability. 18.12.  Static Lines.  To increase speed of closing the ramp if a hostile threat is detected and to prevent damage to the aircraft, the static lines should be stowed after each box goes out.  Do this if the interval between boxes permits safe operation.  Static lines may be cut in an emergency.   228  AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015 Chapter 19 SEARCH AND RESCUE PROCEDURES 19.1.  General.  Most searches are part of an effort to save life; therefore, make every effort to complete  the  search  as  rapidly  and  efficiently  as  possible.    A  thorough  scan  of  the  search  area and accurate navigation significantly increase the probability of detection.  Reactions to sightings must  be  timely  and  accurate.    This  section  outlines  operational  procedures  for  conducting effective  searches  in  the  C-130  aircraft.    Refer  to  the  International  Aeronautical  and  Maritime Search and Rescue Manual (IAMSAR) Volumes I/II for additional information. 19.1.1.  Search  and  Rescue  Satellite-Aided  Tracking  (SARSAT).  SARSAT  is  an international satellite system for SAR.  It consists of a constellation of seven satellites in low earth orbit, 5 geostationary satellites and a network of earth stations, which provide distress alert  and  location  information  to  appropriate  rescue  authorities  anywhere  in  the  world  for users in distress.  The SARSAT satellites detect beacons broadcasting on 406.025 Megahertz (MHz).    The  current  SARSAT  system  consists  of  64  Local  User  Terminals  (LUT)  and  26 Mission  Control  Centers  in  32  countries,  including  12  in  the  United  States.    SARSAT coverage  on  406  MHz  is  world-wide.    The  Rescue  Coordination  Center  (RCC)  at  Langley AFB,  VA,  maintains  one  deployable  LUT  (camper  sized,  air  transportable  van)  to  provide contingency coverage. Table 19.1. Search Altitude (Table 19  7).  Select an optimum search altitude based upon the object of  search,  weather  in  the  search  area,  electronic  or  visual  location  aids  used,  and  any  other known factor, which  would  affect  the search  altitude.  Generally, the lower the search altitude, the  better  the  chance  of  seeing  an  object,  provided  the  altitude  is  not  so  low  that  the  aircraft speed  becomes  detrimental.    During  preliminary  searches,  the  altitude  will  be  higher  to  detect possible signals at greater distances. 19.3.  Search Procedures. 19.3.1.  Rescue  missions  often  involve  commitment  based  upon  calculated  risks.    Give  full consideration to all safety factors.  (T-3)  Timely reaction to all search missions is essential to the safety of personnel or equipment.  Do not jeopardize safety by inadequate preparation or short cuts to expedite takeoff, or arrival at search areas.  Aircrews will comply with all other applicable technical orders and Air Force Instructions, which govern their duties:  (T-2) 19.3.1.1.  Accomplish  complete  pre-departure  flight  planning  except  for  scramble missions.    (T3)    On  scramble  missions,  complete  essential  flight  planning  prior  to  and shortly after takeoff.  (T3) 19.3.1.2.  Use in-flight procedures in accordance with the appropriate theater of operation flight regulations.  (T3) 19.3.1.2.1.  Aircraft  will  maintain  vertical  and  horizontal  separation  in  the  search area.  (T3)  This is critical when several aircraft are searching within close proximity. Table 19.7. Transmit  operation  normal  (position)  reports  as  required  by controlling agency.  (T3) the 19.3.2.  The following general instructions apply to all search missions:  (T3) 19.3.2.1.  Brief crew members that did not attend the operations briefing on the purpose of the mission.  (T3) 19.3.2.2.  Scanners  who  are  not  aircrew  members  will  receive  a  briefing  on  the  search objective.  (T3) 19.3.2.3.  The  PIC  will  supervise  and  coordinate  activities  of  crew  members  during preparation for search, as follows:  (T3) 19.3.2.3.1.  Plan the search with the CSO.  (T3) 19.3.2.3.2.  Discuss with all crew members scanning procedures.  (T3) 19.3.2.3.3.  Discuss  with  the  CSO  and  copilot  the  procedures  for  making  search pattern turns.  (T3) 19.3.2.3.4.  Discuss  the  radio  communication  procedures  with  the  CSO  and  copilot.  (T3) 19.3.2.3.5.  Discuss  the  preparation  of  flares,  sea  dyes,  and  smoke  signals  for deployment with the loadmaster.  (T3) 19.3.2.3.6.  Discuss the preparation of personnel and equipment for airborne delivery with  the  loadmaster  and  pararescuemen.    (T3)    Ensure  all  crew  members  are thoroughly  familiar  with  the  checklists  to  be  used  for  various  airborne  delivery options.  (T3) 19.3.2.4.  The  PIC  will  coordinate  crew  member  activities  during  prosecution  of  the search by:  (T3) 19.3.2.4.1.  Ensuring  completion  of  the  Search  Checklist  prior  to  commencing  any low-level search.  (T3) 19.3.2.4.2.  Alerting  the  scanners  to  begin  scanning  when  approaching  the  search area.  (T3) 19.3.2.4.3.  Directing crew members to put on their LPUs prior to  descending below 2,000 feet overwater.  (T3) 19.3.2.4.4.  Checking with crew members to  ensure all equipment  and personnel  are ready for action in the event of a sighting.  (T3) 19.3.2.4.5.  Making  periodic  checks  of  fuel  remaining  to  ensure  sufficient  fuel remains for return to home station.  (T3) AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015   233  19.3.2.5.  Report  all  deviations  from  assigned  search  procedures  to  the  on-scene commander or mission commander.  (T3) 19.3.2.6.  Thoroughly  investigate  sightings  and  report  findings  immediately.    Initiate recovery  action  or  assistance  when  the  survivors  are  located  and  inform  appropriate agencies of the progress.  (T3) 19.3.2.7.  The CSO will:  (T3) 19.3.2.7.1.  Maintain an accurate record of area searched.  (T3) 19.3.2.7.2.  Direct the aircraft to ensure proper coverage of the search area.  (T3) 19.3.2.7.3.  Continually  cross-check  headings,  drift,  time,  airspeed,  and  altitude  to maintain the best possible search coverage.  (T3) 19.3.2.7.4.  Use  a  large-scale  chart  when  searching  over  land  to  ensure  terrain  and obstacle clearance.  Use a search pattern graph as a suitable reference while searching overwater.  (T3) 19.3.2.7.5.  Record sighting information on log and plot position on navigation chart.  (T3) 19.3.2.7.6.  Monitor equipment during radar and electronic searches.  (T3) 19.3.2.7.7.  Debrief  controlling  agency  with  an  accurate  depiction  of  any  areas searched.  (T3) 19.3.2.7.8.  When other aircraft are involved, track and assign search areas for other aircraft.  (T3) 19.3.2.8.  The  PIC  will  insure  radio  communications  are  maintained  with  other  search aircraft and the controlling agency.  (T3) 19.3.2.9.  The  loadmaster  will  assume  supervision  of  the  scanners  and  will  schedule rotation and rest periods.  Scanners should be rotated every 30 to 45 minutes of scanning and rested after two hours, if enough scanners are available.  (T3) 19.3.2.10.  All crew members will assist with the scanning duties when possible.  (T3) 19.3.2.11.  When a search is completed with negative results, consider searching the area again.  Normally, position subsequent search legs between or 45° to the previous search legs.  This procedure, in effect, results in smaller track spacing. 19.3.3.  In the search area:  (T3) 19.3.3.1.  Descend to search altitude (Table 19.7).  (T3) 19.3.3.2.  Have  CSO  obtain  the  wind  at  search  altitude    (T3)    o  visually  cover  the  area thoroughly,  slow  the  airspeed  during  search  patterns  (refer  to  the  Aircraft  Performance Manual).  (T3) 19.3.3.3.  Notify ATC of arrival on scene and estimated endurance.  (T3) 19.3.3.4.  Vector other aircraft to the scene.  (T3) 19.3.3.5.  Use radar (some life rafts carry reflectors).  (T3)   234  AFI11-2EC-130JV3  8 APRIL 2015 19.3.3.6.  Assume  on-scene  command  until  relieved  by  another  duly  appointed  SAR aircraft.  (T3)  As other aircraft arrive, do the following:  (T3) 19.3.3.6.1.  Establish contact on channels other than Guard.  (T3) 19.3.3.6.2.  Obtain aircraft type identification, endurance, and rescue capability.  (T3) 19.3.3.6.3.  Assign  altimeter  setting,  frequencies,  search  areas,  patterns,  and  altitude separation.  (T3) 19.3.3.7.  If leaving the search area because of lack of fuel, assign another aircraft as on-scene commander.  (T3) 19.3.4.  Determine  an  accurate  wind  prior  to  and  throughout  the  search.    (T3)    Use  Table 19.9 to estimate wind velocity near the surface. 19.3.5.  When  flying  search  patterns,  turns  must  be  accurate  and  uniform  as  possible.    The following procedures are provided to assist the aircrew in making precise turns: 19.3.5.1.  Fly the aircraft on autopilot when possible. 19.3.5.2.  Prior  to  starting  the  pattern,  the  CSO  will  brief  the  crew  on  the  direction  and rate of turn and the turn command procedure used. 19.3.5.3.  Normally, all turns are standard rate (3° per second) unless track spacing is less in  distance  than  the  diameter  of  a  standard  turn  at  a  given  speed.    In  this  situation, continue turn to 180° and adjust turn rate as required to remain within the pattern. Table 19.8.  Sector Search Pattern Computation.
